### INTRODUCTION ESSAY (already written for this psalm)
# Introduction Essay

Psalm 18 stands apart in the Psalter through a feature that immediately arrests scholarly attention: it exists in duplicate. The identical poem appears again in 2 Samuel 22, positioned as David's victory hymn after years of conflict. This textual doubling is not mere repetition but a window into the psalm's transmission history. Scholars analyzing the approximately 129 variants between the two Masoretic versions have identified distinct editorial channels—one liturgical and regulated (the Psalter), the other narrative and perhaps more archival (Samuel). The version in 2 Samuel 22 generally preserves "rougher," more archaic readings, while Psalm 18 exhibits signs of liturgical smoothing intended for corporate worship. The most telling variant occurs in the closing doxology (v. 50/51), where 2 Samuel reads *migdol* (מִגְדּוֹל, "Tower of salvation") and Psalm 18 reads *magdil* (מַגְדִּיל, "He who magnifies salvation"). This variation has transcended academic debate to shape Jewish liturgical practice: in the Grace After Meals, *Migdol* is recited on days when the Musaf offering was brought—Shabbat and Festivals—while *Magdil* is recited on weekdays, suggesting the *Migdol* reading (Tower) may be original, with *Magdil* (Magnifies) representing a later dynamic verbal interpretation common in praise poetry.

The orthographic analysis pioneered by Frank Moore Cross and David Noel Freedman anchors the poem's core in the tenth century BCE. The defective spelling preserved in 2 Samuel 22—with fewer vowel letters (*matres lectionis*)—resembles the orthography of early Hebrew inscriptions like the Gezer Calendar. The survival of archaic pronominal suffixes (e.g., *-h* for "his" instead of *-o*) and specific verbal forms that predate standard Biblical Hebrew strongly counter arguments for late post-exilic composition. This textual archaeology matters because it grounds the psalm in the height of the United Monarchy, when Israel was actively negotiating its theological identity against Canaanite religious culture.

The psalm's genre is complex, combining elements of royal thanksgiving, victory hymn, and theophanic display. But its most striking feature is what I call "double narration"—the same deliverance is recounted twice using radically different rhetorical modes. The first narrative (vv. 4–31) is mythic and vertical: God descends from the transcendent realm in cosmic upheaval. The earth quakes, smoke pours from divine nostrils, fire devours from God's mouth, cherubim serve as divine transport. This is not reportage but poetry that appropriates Ancient Near Eastern storm-god imagery. The second narrative (vv. 32–50) is martial and horizontal: the king, empowered by God, actively pursues enemies. "I pursued my enemies and overtook them" (אֶרְדּוֹף אוֹיְבַי וְאַשִּׂיגֵם, v. 38). "I struck them down, and they could rise no more" (אֶמְחָצֵם וְלֹא־יֻכְלוּ קוּם, v. 39). The shift from passive recipient to active agent is complete.

This double structure creates theological synthesis: victory is entirely God's work (Panel I), yet it is enacted through human agency (Panel II). The pivot between these panels is the righteousness section (vv. 20–27), where the king claims, "The LORD rewarded me according to my righteousness" (יִגְמְלֵנִי ה׳ כְּצִדְקִי, v. 21). This assertion has troubled readers from the Rabbis to modern scholars. How does David's claim of clean hands (כְּבֹר יָדַי, v. 21) square with his documented sins? The answer lies in recognizing royal ideology: the king speaks not as private individual but as covenant mediator. His righteousness is relational—keeping faith with the covenant statutes (vv. 21–23), not claiming moral perfection. The Midrash asks why Saul is separated from "all his enemies" in the superscription (v. 1) and answers: Saul was unique, not a foreign pagan but "the Anointed of the Lord." David's victory over Saul was one of restraint and survival, not conquest—a righteousness of not killing the Lord's anointed when given opportunity.

The theophanic center (vv. 8–16) demands attention for its polemical appropriation of Canaanite imagery. In the Ugaritic Baal Cycle, Baal holds the epithet *rkb ʿrpt* ("Rider on the Clouds"). He wields thunder, lightning, and rain to defeat cosmic enemies Yam (Sea) and Mot (Death). Psalm 18:10–11 co-opts this imagery directly: the LORD "rode upon a cherub and flew; He glided on the wings of the wind" (וַיִּרְכַּב עַל־כְּרוּב וַיָּעֹף וַיֵּדֶא עַל־כַּנְפֵי־רוּחַ). The psalm's description of God's arsenal—"hailstones and coals of fire" (בָּרָד וְגַחֲלֵי־אֵשׁ, v. 13), arrows of lightning (v. 14)—mirrors the iconography of the Ancient Near Eastern storm god. But the poet demythologizes this conflict. For David, the "floods of Belial" (נַחֲלֵי בְלִיַּעַל, v. 5) and "snares of death" (מוֹקְשֵׁי מָוֶת, v. 6) are not literal cosmic deities but metaphors for historical enemies and political chaos. Belial—whether proper name or personification of chaos—becomes in this psalm a descriptor of overwhelming threat. The message is clear: it is the LORD, not Baal, who commands the storm; it is the LORD, not any other deity, who rides the cherubim.

The lexical research illuminates several cruxes. The opening declaration, "I love You, O LORD, my strength" (אֶרְחָמְךָ ה׳ חִזְקִי, v. 2), is entirely absent from 2 Samuel 22, suggesting later addition to soften the martial tone. The verb *rḥm* in the Qal stem describing a human's love for God is a *hapax legomenon*. Usually appearing in the Piel and describing superior's love for inferior, its appearance here in the Qal is striking. Some scholars detect Aramaic influence; others argue it represents intense "womb-love" or "gut-love" (*rechem*, womb)—a desperate, visceral dependence on God fitting the context of deliverance from death.

The "horn of salvation" (קֶרֶן־יִשְׁעִי, v. 3) combines animal/royal imagery. In Ancient Near Eastern iconography, horns symbolize power and kingship. When applied to God, the metaphor asserts divine strength as the source of royal deliverance. The concordance shows this precise phrase appearing only here and in 2 Samuel 22:3, but similar "horn" imagery for power appears throughout scripture (Deut 33:17, Ps 89:18, 92:11). The phrase entered Jewish liturgy as part of *Ein Keloheinu*, where it stands in a catena of verses affirming God's uniqueness: "For who is God save for the LORD? And who is a Rock except our God?" (כִּי מִי אֱלוֹהַּ מִבַּלְעֲדֵי ה׳ וּמִי צוּר זוּלָתִי אֱלֹהֵינוּ, v. 32).

The most interpretively vexed phrase is "Your humility makes me great" (וְעַנְוַתְךָ תַרְבֵּנִי, v. 36). The term *ʿanwah* can mean humility, care, or condescension. Radak and classical commentators favor "condescension"—God stoops from transcendence to intervene for the king. Malbim offers psychological reading: different synonyms like *Tzur* (strong rock) and *Sela* (cleft rock) represent different states of trust. Modern translations often choose "care," emphasizing divine attention rather than divine self-lowering. The ambiguity is productive: the phrase holds together God's transcendence and immanence—the One who is high must bend low to save.

The climax presents international submission: "Foreigners came cringing to me; they heard, and they obeyed me" (לְשֵׁמַע אֹזֶן יִשָּׁמְעוּ לִי בְּנֵי־נֵכָר יְכַחֲשׁוּ־לִי, v. 45). The verb *kḥš* is debated—does it mean "cringe," "lie," or "grow lean"? The context suggests submission through fear, but Ibn Ezra proposes they deny allegiance to former allies because of David. This international scope transcends the superscription's claim of deliverance from Saul, suggesting the psalm "telescopes" David's entire reign—or represents liturgical/eschatological expansion. The Haftarah tradition pairs this with the Song of the Sea (Exodus 15) on the Seventh Day of Passover, juxtaposing the two archetypal redemptions of Jewish history: deliverance from Egypt (national) and deliverance of David (royal/messianic).

The final verse clinches the royal theology: "He who accords great victories to His king, keeps faith with His anointed, with David and his offspring forever" (מַגְדִּיל יְשׁוּעוֹת מַלְכּוֹ וְעֹשֶׂה חֶסֶד לִמְשִׁיחוֹ לְדָוִד וּלְזַרְעוֹ עַד־עוֹלָם, v. 51). The term *mashiaḥ* (anointed) here refers to David and his dynasty, but the "forever" clause opened this text to messianic interpretation. Radak, in explicit anti-Christian polemic, insists *zera* (seed) implies plural biological descendants of the historical dynasty. Christian readers, from Luther to Calvin, saw the psalm as typological—David prefiguring Christ. Luther found in "the cords of death" (חֶבְלֵי־מָוֶת, v. 5) Christ's agony in Gethsemane, and in the "darkness" of theophany (v. 11) the *Deus Absconditus*, the Hidden God who works salvation *sub contrario*—under the appearance of opposites.

What earned this psalm its canonical place? It synthesizes Israel's foundational theological claims: the storm god is Israel's covenant Lord; military victory flows from moral fidelity; the king is God's empowered agent yet derives all power from God; personal deliverance foreshadows national and cosmic redemption. The poem moves from individual crisis to cosmic theophany to international dominion to eternal dynasty—each level containing and transcending the previous. It is not merely David's song but the paradigmatic royal song, establishing how one king's deliverance becomes foundation for an everlasting kingdom. The liturgical insertion of the opening "I love You" (*erḥāmĕkā*) in the Psalter version shows how later tradition sought to universalize this martial victory hymn into personal devotion—a move that secured its place not just in royal ceremony but in the private prayer of every Jew who cries from distress and experiences deliverance.

## Modern Jewish Liturgical Use

Psalm 18 is not recited in its entirety as a fixed part of standard daily liturgy, but its verses and phrases have become deeply embedded in Jewish prayer across multiple traditions. The most prominent liturgical use occurs in the Grace After Meals (*Birkat HaMazon*), where verse 51—"He accords great victories to His king, keeps faith with His anointed, with David and his offspring forever" (מַגְדִּיל יְשׁוּעוֹת מַלְכּוֹ וְעֹשֶׂה חֶסֶד לִמְשִׁיחוֹ לְדָוִד וּלְזַרְעוֹ עַד־עוֹלָם)—serves as the climactic conclusion to the *HaRachaman* supplications. This placement transforms the meal's end into a reaffirmation of Israel's eschatological hope, directly linking the messianic promise to daily sustenance. Remarkably, the *migdol/magdil* textual variant shapes ritual practice: the 2 Samuel reading *Migdol* ("Tower of") is recited on Shabbat and Festivals to enhance the celebratory tone, while the Psalm 18 reading *Magdil* ("He magnifies") is used on weekdays.

Verse 32—"For who is God save for the LORD? And who is a Rock except our God?" (כִּי מִי אֱלוֹהַּ מִבַּלְעֲדֵי ה׳ וּמִי צוּר זוּלָתִי אֱלֹהֵינוּ)—appears in multiple liturgical contexts. In Ashkenazic and Sephardic traditions, it forms part of the scriptural prelude to *Ein Keloheinu*, creating a powerful chain of verses affirming monotheism. The Edot HaMizrach tradition incorporates verses 31–32 into the Torah service, proclaiming during the procession: "The way of God is perfect; the word of the LORD is pure; He is a shield for all who seek refuge in Him. For who is God save for the LORD?" (הָאֵל תָּמִים דַּרְכּוֹ אִמְרַת־ה׳ צְרוּפָה מָגֵן הוּא לְכֹל הַחֹסִים בּוֹ כִּי מִי אֱלוֹהַּ מִבַּלְעֲדֵי ה׳). This usage reframes the Torah reading as an encounter with the same divine perfection and protection celebrated in David's victory hymn, transforming royal thanksgiving into communal covenant affirmation. Individual phrases also appear in specialized contexts: verse 46 ("The LORD lives, and blessed be my Rock") occurs in various *Pesukei d'Zimra* traditions, while verses from the theophany section are recited in the mystical *Perek Shirah* as the "songs" of the Clouds of Glory and the Sword. The liturgical reception reveals how Jewish tradition read this royal psalm through increasingly universal and messianic lenses, making David's personal deliverance a template for national redemption.

### MACRO THESIS
**Thesis**: Psalm 18 functions as a theological charter for Davidic kingship, integrating cosmic theophany, personal righteousness, and military triumph into a unified narrative of divinely legitimated royal power. The psalm's dramatic movement from individual crisis through spectacular divine intervention to international dominion establishes the paradigm by which one king's deliverance becomes the foundation for an eternal dynasty. The tension between divine agency (the cosmic warrior theophany of vv. 8-16) and human merit (the righteousness claims of vv. 21-25 and military prowess of vv. 38-43) is not contradiction but royal ideology: the anointed king is simultaneously God's empowered agent and righteous recipient of divine favor.
**Genre**: Royal Thanksgiving Psalm / Victory Song. This is a complex genre combining elements of thanksgiving (individual deliverance), hymnic praise (theophanic description), wisdom reflection (vv. 26-28), and royal ideology (concluding with eternal dynasty promise). The psalm's dual appearance in Psalms and 2 Samuel 22 marks it as a defining statement of Davidic theology rather than merely liturgical material.
**Context**: The superscription explicitly dates this to David's deliverance from Saul and his enemies, likely intended to represent the consolidation of David's kingdom after years of conflict. However, its literary sophistication and theological scope suggest it served as a paradigmatic royal psalm for the Davidic dynasty, celebrating not just one victory but establishing the theological pattern of divinely-empowered kingship. The heavy use of Canaanite/Ugaritic storm-god imagery (Baal traditions) shows Israel appropriating ANE royal ideology while subordinating it to YHWH. The movement from personal enemies to international dominion (vv. 44-46) and eternal dynasty (v. 51) suggests use in royal coronation or victory celebrations, functioning to legitimate each successive Davidic king as heir to divine promises.

**Structure**:
  - v. 1: Superscription: Historical framing
  - vv. 2-3: Opening declaration and epithet cascade
  - vv. 4-6: Crisis: Death's assault described
  - v. 7: Turning point: The cry heard
  - vv. 8-16: Theophany: God's dramatic cosmic intervention
  - vv. 17-20: Personal rescue enacted
  - vv. 21-25: Righteousness claims: Merit as basis for deliverance
  - vv. 26-28: Wisdom interlude: Principle of divine reciprocity
  - vv. 29-31: Transitional confidence: Light, strength, and divine perfection
  - vv. 32-37: Divine empowerment for battle
  - vv. 38-43: Battle victory: First-person military triumph
  - vv. 44-46: International dominion: From personal to cosmic scale
  - vv. 47-49: Renewed praise: Doxology grounded in victory
  - vv. 50-51: Vow of praise and dynastic promise

**Poetic Devices**:
  - Epithet cascade/piling: Rapid accumulation of divine titles and metaphors without development - creating overwhelming sense of God's multifaceted protection
  - Mythological personification: Death (Mavet), Belial, and Sheol portrayed as active agents with weapons (ropes, torrents, snares) attacking the psalmist
  - Theophanic imagery (storm theophany): Extended description of God's appearance using storm, fire, earthquake, volcanic, and weather phenomena drawn from Canaanite Baal mythology but applied to YHWH
  - Anthropomorphism (somatic imagery): God's body parts as active agents: nostrils smoking, mouth breathing fire, hands reaching down, ears hearing, eyes seeing, right hand sustaining
  - Repetition and refrain: Key terms recur throughout: 'enemies' (9x), 'deliver/rescue' (yasha'/natsal), 'rock' (tsur/sela), 'hand/hands,' 'LORD' (YHWH - 19x)
  - Chiastic movement: The psalm's structure moves from crisis inward to divine center and back out to resolution: Crisis (vv. 4-6) → Call (v. 7) → Divine Descent (vv. 8-16) → Rescue (vv. 17-20) → Reflection (vv. 21-31) → Empowerment (vv. 32-37) → Victory (vv. 38-46) → Praise (vv. 47-51)
  - Tense shifting (verbal aspect): Movement from past narrative (vv. 4-6: 'encompassed,' 'terrified'), to present perfect (v. 7: 'I called'), to future/habitual (vv. 26-28: general principles), back to past victory (vv. 38-43), to present praise (vv. 47-51)
  - Synonymous parallelism: Dominant throughout - second line restates/reinforces first with variation: 'In my distress I called on the LORD / cried out to my God' (v. 7); 'The earth rocked and quaked / the foundations of mountains shook' (v. 8)
  - Merism: Completeness expressed through polar opposites or comprehensive categories: 'all his enemies' (v. 1), earth and mountains (v. 8), sky and beneath feet (v. 10), loyal-blameless-pure vs. perverse (vv. 26-27), lowly folk and haughty eyes (v. 28)
  - Metaphoric vehicle clustering: Multiple metaphors drawn from similar semantic domains: military (fortress, shield), geological (rock, crag), spatial (haven, refuge, heights), animal (deer), light (lamp, darkness)
  - Hyperbolic intensification: Exaggerated imagery for rhetorical effect: smoke from nostrils, fire from mouth, sky bending down, riding cherubim, grinding enemies to dust, scaling walls
  - First-person testimony: Sustained 'I' narration throughout creates personal witness account rather than third-person report. The first person intensifies in battle section (vv. 38-43) where 'I' is subject of nearly every verb
  - Inclusio (envelope structure): Opening and closing frame created through repetition of key themes: 'servant of the LORD... David' (v. 1) // 'His anointed... David and his offspring forever' (v. 51); 'delivered from enemies and Saul' (v. 1) // 'rescued from enemies' (v. 49); epithets at beginning (vv. 2-3) reprised at end (v. 47)

**Research Questions** (from Macro Analyst):
  1. How do the specific theophanic elements in vv. 8-16 (cherub-riding, cloud-chariot, storm imagery) compare to Ugaritic Baal texts and other ANE divine warrior traditions? What does Israel's appropriation of Canaanite imagery reveal about polemical theology?
  2. What is the significance of 'Belial' (בְלִיַּעַל) in v. 5, and how does this term function in its biblical and extra-biblical contexts? Is it a proper name (deity), personification of chaos, or metaphor for destruction?
  3. How should the righteousness claims in vv. 21-25 be interpreted within broader biblical theology? Do they represent royal ideology, conditional covenant theology, or pre-exilic understanding later nuanced by prophetic/wisdom traditions?
  4. What is the textual relationship between Psalm 18 and 2 Samuel 22? What are the significant variants, and what do they reveal about transmission, liturgical adaptation, or theological development?
  5. How does the cherub imagery in v. 11 relate to ark theology, temple decoration, and broader ANE concepts of divine transport? What does God 'riding a cherub' signify about divine mobility and accessibility?
  6. What is the precise meaning and function of 'horn of salvation' (קֶרֶן־יִשְׁעִי) in v. 3? How does this royal/animal metaphor work in Davidic ideology and later messianic interpretation?
  7. How does the 'mighty waters' (מַיִם רַבִּים) imagery in vv. 5, 17 relate to creation theology, chaos tradition (Tehom/Rahab/Leviathan), and exodus typology? What is the polyvalent significance of water as threat and deliverance?
  8. What is the function of the wisdom-style section (vv. 26-28) within a royal victory psalm? Does this represent genre mixing, or is there an integrated royal-wisdom ideology in monarchy period?
  9. How should contemporary readers approach the graphic violence in vv. 38-43 ('ground them fine as dust')? What is the relationship between ancient victory rhetoric, actual warfare practices, and theological interpretation?
  10. What is the semantic range of 'anointed' (מָשִׁיחַ, mashiach) in v. 51, and how did this psalm function in developing messianic expectations? How does historical David relate to eschatological Messiah?
  11. How does the international dominion language (vv. 44-46, 50) function - is this imperial propaganda, theological vision of Davidic covenant universalism, or eschatological hope? What is the relationship to ANE imperial rhetoric?
  12. What is the structure and progression (if any) of the nine epithets in vv. 2-3? Do they move from geological to military to spatial, or is the piling effect itself the rhetorical strategy without internal logic?
  13. How does the 'lamp/darkness' imagery in v. 29 relate to creation ('let there be light'), wisdom literature's knowledge/ignorance, and royal ideology? Is this about moral guidance, military victory, or cosmic order?
  14. What is the meaning of 'Your care/humility made me great' (עַנְוַתְךָ תַרְבֵּנִי) in v. 36? The term ענוה can mean humility, condescension, or care - which reading fits the context, and what does divine 'humility' mean theologically?
  15. How do the perfect/imperfect verb forms pattern through the psalm, and what does verbal aspect contribute to understanding the temporal relationship between crisis, theophany, rescue, and ongoing praise?
  16. What is the relationship between the psalm's superscription's historical claim (delivered from Saul) and the international dominion of vv. 44-46? Does the psalm telescope David's entire reign, or is the ending liturgical/eschatological expansion?
  17. How does the 'cleanness of hands' (בֹר יָדַי) in vv. 21, 25 relate to ritual purity, ethical conduct, and royal ideology? Is this about ceremonial qualification for divine audience or moral righteousness?
  18. What is the significance of God hearing from 'His temple' (הֵיכָל) in v. 7? Is this the earthly Jerusalem temple (anachronistic for David's early reign), heavenly temple, or cosmic palace? How does temple theology intersect royal theology?

### MICRO DISCOVERIES
**Verse 1**
**Phonetic**: `lam-nats-**TSĒ**-aḥ  lə-**ʿE**-vedh yə-hō-**WĀH** lə-dhā-**WIDH** 'a-**SHER** di-**BER**  lay-hō-**WĀH** 'eth-div-**RĒY** hash-shiy-**RĀH** haz-**ZŌ'**-th bə-**YŌM** hits-tsiy-l-yə-hō-wāh-'ō-**THŌ** mi-**KAF** kol-'ō-yə-**VĀY**-w ū-miy-**YADH** shā-**'ŪL**`
Elaborate superscription establishing historical frame and double identity of David as both 'servant of the LORD' (עֶבֶד יְהֹוָה) and royal figure. The phrase 'servant of the LORD' is rare in psalm titles - why this specific title here? The dual rescue ('from hands of ALL enemies AND from hand of Saul') creates hierarchy of threat.
  Lexical: לְעֶבֶד יְהֹוָה, דִּבְרֵי הַשִּׁירָה הַזֹּאת, מִכַּף כׇּל־אֹיְבָיו וּמִיַּד שָׁאוּל
  Figurative: 'hand' as metonymy for power/threat

**Verse 2**
**Phonetic**: `way-yō'-**MAR** 'er-ḥom-**KHĀ** yə-hō-**WĀH** ḥiz-**QIY**`
Opening with visceral love language - 'אֶרְחׇמְךָ' from רחם (womb-love, deep compassion). This is passionate, emotional opening, not formal liturgical praise. The verb is rare in direct address to God. Single divine epithet 'my strength' (חִזְקִי) after the intensely personal verb.
  Lexical: אֶרְחׇמְךָ, חִזְקִי
  Figurative: strength as personified divine attribute

**Verse 3**
**Phonetic**: `yə-hō-**WĀH**  sal-**ʿIY** ū-mə-tsū-dhā-**THIY** ū-mə-fal-**TIY** 'ē-**LIY** tsū-**RIY** 'e-ḥe-seh-**BŌ** **MĀ**-ghin-niy wə-qe-ren-yish-**ʿIY** mis-ga-**BIY**`
Epithet cascade of NINE divine titles/metaphors in rapid succession without development. Mixing geological (rock, crag), military (shield, fortress), and spatial (refuge, haven) imagery. The phrase 'קֶרֶן־יִשְׁעִי' (horn of my salvation) is striking - animal/royal imagery. Why 'horn'?
  Lexical: סַלְעִי, קֶרֶן־יִשְׁעִי, מִשְׂגַּבִּי
  Figurative: rock/fortress cluster = immovability, shield/horn cluster = protection, horn = strength/power, possibly royal

**Verse 4**
**Phonetic**: `mə-hul-**LĀL** 'eq-**RĀ'** yə-hō-**WĀH** ū-min-'ō-yə-**VAY** 'i-ū-**SHĒ**-aʿ`
Brief praise formula linking call and deliverance. The participle 'מְהֻלָּל' (worthy of praise) is interesting - passive/reflexive form suggesting inherent praiseworthiness. Tight causality: called → delivered. Sets up crisis narrative to come.
  Lexical: מְהֻלָּל, אִוָּשֵׁעַ
  Figurative: calling as trigger for divine action

**Verse 5**
**Phonetic**: `'a-fā-**FŪ**-niy ḥev-lēy-**MĀ**-weth wə-na-ḥa-**LĒY** və-liy-**YA**-ʿal yə-va-ʿa-**THŪ**-niy`
Shift to cosmic/mythological crisis language. 'חֶבְלֵי־מָוֶת' (cords/ropes of Death) personifies Death as active agent with weapons. 'נַחֲלֵי בְלִיַּעַל' - Belial as proper name or abstract chaos? The verbs אָפַף (encompass) and בָּעַת (terrify) create claustrophobic threat.
  Lexical: חֶבְלֵי־מָוֶת, נַחֲלֵי בְלִיַּעַל, יְבַעֲתוּנִי
  Figurative: Death with ropes = hunter metaphor, Belial with torrents = flood/chaos metaphor

**Verse 6**
**Phonetic**: `ḥev-**LĒY** shə-**'ŌL** sə-vā-**VŪ**-niy qid-**MŪ**-niy **MŌ**-qə-shēy **MĀ**-weth`
Parallelism continues mythological language. 'חֶבְלֵי שְׁאוֹל' (cords of Sheol) and 'מוֹקְשֵׁי מָוֶת' (snares of Death) - both underworld and Death have binding/trapping tools. Three verbs of encirclement: סָבַב (encircled), קָדַם (confronted), creating total siege imagery.
  Lexical: חֶבְלֵי שְׁאוֹל, מוֹקְשֵׁי מָוֶת, קִדְּמוּנִי
  Figurative: ropes and snares = Death/Sheol as hunters, total encirclement = no escape

**Verse 7**
**Phonetic**: `bats-tsar-**LIY**  'eq-**RĀ'** yə-hō-**WĀH** wə-'el-'e-lō-**HAY** 'a-sha-ū-**Aʿ** yish-**MAʿ** mē-hēy-khā-**LŌ** qō-**LIY** wə-shaw-ʿā-**THIY** lə-fā-**NĀY**-w  tā-**VŌ'** və-'oz-**NĀY**-w`
PIVOT verse - shift from crisis to cry. Double call formula: 'אֶקְרָא יְהֹוָה' (I called on LORD) and 'אֲשַׁוֵּעַ' (I cried out). God hears 'מֵהֵיכָלוֹ' (from His temple) - cosmic temple or earthly? The phrase 'לְפָנָיו תָּבוֹא בְאׇזְנָיו' (before Him it came into His ears) is striking - the cry has spatial journey into divine presence.
  Lexical: בַּצַּר־לִי, מֵהֵיכָלוֹ, וְשַׁוְעָתִי לְפָנָיו תָּבוֹא בְאׇזְנָיו
  Figurative: temple as cosmic communication center, ears personify divine receptivity

**Verse 8**
**Phonetic**: `wa-tigh-**ʿASH** wa-tir-**ʿASH**  hā-**'Ā**-rets ū-mō-sə-**DHĒY** hā-**RIY**-m yir-**GĀ**-zū way-yith-gā-ʿa-**SHŪ** kiy-**ḤĀ**-rāh **LŌ**`
THEOPHANY begins with earthquake. Four verbs of shaking: תִּגְעַשׁ, תִּרְעַשׁ, יִרְגָּזוּ, יִּתְגָּֽעֲשׁוּ - earth shakes, mountains' foundations shake. Reason clause: 'כִּי־חָרָה לוֹ' (because anger burned to Him) - God's anger as cause of cosmic disturbance. Why anger?
  Lexical: וַתִּגְעַשׁ וַתִּרְעַשׁ הָאָרֶץ, וּמוֹסְדֵי הָרִים, כִּי־חָרָה לוֹ
  Figurative: earthquake as divine anger manifestation, cosmic architecture (foundations) made unstable

**Verse 9**
**Phonetic**: `**ʿĀ**-lāh ʿā-**SHĀN**  bə-'a-**PŌ** wə-'ēsh-mi-**PIY**-w tō'-**KHĒL** ge-ḥā-**LIY**-m bā-ʿa-**RŪ** mim-**MEN**-nū`
Intensely anthropomorphic God - smoke from nostrils, fire from mouth, coals blazing from Him. Three sources of fire/heat from divine body. The verb בָּעֲרוּ (blazed) connects to חָרָה (burned) in v.8 - divine anger is literal fire.
  Lexical: עָלָה עָשָׁן בְּאַפּוֹ, וְאֵשׁ־מִפִּיו תֹּאכֵל, גֶּחָלִים בָּעֲרוּ מִמֶּנּוּ
  Figurative: divine anger as literal fire, God as dragon/volcano figure

**Verse 10**
**Phonetic**: `way-**YĒT** shā-ma-**YIM** way-yē-**RADH** wa-ʿa-rā-**FEL** **TA**-ḥath ragh-**LĀY**-w`
God bends/bows sky and descends. 'וַיֵּט שָׁמַיִם' - sky as flexible material God manipulates. 'עֲרָפֶל תַּחַת רַגְלָיו' (thick darkness under His feet) - God stands on darkness/cloud. Spatial descent from cosmic realm toward earth.
  Lexical: וַיֵּט שָׁמַיִם, עֲרָפֶל, תַּחַת רַגְלָיו
  Figurative: sky as tent/curtain God manipulates, darkness as divine transport or concealment

**Verse 11**
**Phonetic**: `way-yir-**KAV** ʿal-kə-**RŪV** way-yā-**ʿŌF** way-**YĒ**-dhe' ʿal-kan-fēy-**RŪ**-aḥ`
God rides cherub and flies on wind wings. 'וַיִּרְכַּב עַל־כְּרוּב' - cherub as divine mount (singular, not plural). 'וַיֵּדֶא עַל־כַּנְפֵי־רוּחַ' - gliding on wind's wings. Two verbs of motion: רכב (ride/mount) and דאה (glide/soar - rare verb).
  Lexical: וַיִּרְכַּב עַל־כְּרוּב, וַיֵּדֶא, עַל־כַּנְפֵי־רוּחַ
  Figurative: cherub as divine vehicle, wind with wings - nature personification

**Verse 12**
**Phonetic**: `yā-sheth-**ḤŌ**-shekh  sith-**RŌ** sə-**VIY**-vō-thāy-w su-kā-**THŌ** ḥesh-khath-**MA**-yim ʿā-**VĒY** shə-ḥā-**QIY**-m`
God makes darkness His hiding place. 'סִתְרוֹ' (His hiding/concealment) and 'סֻכָּתוֹ' (His booth/pavilion) - darkness as divine tent/shelter. Triple description: darkness, dark waters, thick clouds of skies. Why does God hide in darkness?
  Lexical: יָשֶׁת־חֹשֶׁךְ סִתְרוֹ, סְבִיבוֹתָיו סֻכָּתוֹ, חֶשְׁכַת־מַיִם עָבֵי שְׁחָקִים
  Figurative: darkness as divine garment/shelter, clouds as tent structure

**Verse 13**
**Phonetic**: `min-**NŌ**-ghah-h negh-**DŌ** ʿā-**VĀY**-w ʿā-və-**RŪ** bā-**RĀDH** wə-gha-ḥa-lēy-**'ĒSH**`
Shift from darkness to brightness. 'מִנֹּגַהּ נֶגְדּוֹ' (from brightness before Him) - light precedes God. 'עָבָיו עָבְרוּ' (His clouds passed through) - clouds move/pass. Then 'בָּרָד וְגַחֲלֵי־אֵשׁ' (hail and coals of fire) - violent weather phenomena.
  Lexical: מִנֹּגַהּ נֶגְדּוֹ, עָבָיו עָבְרוּ, בָּרָד וְגַחֲלֵי־אֵשׁ
  Figurative: brightness as divine herald, clouds as divine army

**Verse 14**
**Phonetic**: `way-yar-**ʿĒM** bash-shā-**MA**-yim  yə-hō-**WĀH** wə-ʿel-**YŌN** yi-**TĒN** qō-**LŌ** bā-**RĀDH** wə-gha-ḥa-lēy-**'ĒSH**`
God's voice as thunder. 'וַיַּרְעֵם בַּשָּׁמַיִם יְהֹוָה' - LORD thunders in heavens. Double divine name: יְהֹוָה and עֶלְיוֹן (Most High). 'יִתֵּן קֹלוֹ' (gave His voice) - thunder IS divine speech. Repeated hail and fire phrase from v.13.
  Lexical: וַיַּרְעֵם בַּשָּׁמַיִם יְהֹוָה, וְעֶלְיוֹן יִתֵּן קֹלוֹ
  Figurative: thunder as divine speech, weather phenomena as divine weapons

**Verse 15**
**Phonetic**: `way-yish-**LAḤ** ḥits-tsāy-**W** way-fiy-**TSĒM** ū-və-rā-**QIY**-m **RĀV** way-hum-**MĒM**`
God shoots arrows and scatters enemies. 'וַיִּשְׁלַח חִצָּיו' - sends His arrows. Lightning as arrows: 'וּבְרָקִים רָב' (lightning, many). Two verbs of enemy defeat: פוץ (scatter) and המם (confuse/rout). Military metaphors for weather phenomena.
  Lexical: וַיִּשְׁלַח חִצָּיו, וּבְרָקִים רָב, וַיְהֻמֵּם
  Figurative: lightning as divine archery, storm as battle

**Verse 16**
**Phonetic**: `way-yē-rā-**'Ū**  'a-**FIY**-qēy **MA**-yim **WAY**-yi-gā-lū mō-sə-**DHŌTH** tē-**VĒL** mi-ga-ʿa-rā-thə-**KHĀ** yə-hō-**WĀH** min-nish-**MATH** **RŪ**-aḥ 'a-**PE**-khā`
Cosmic revelation of deep waters and earth foundations. 'וַיֵּרָאוּ אֲפִיקֵי מַיִם' (channels of waters were seen) and 'וַיִּגָּלוּ מוֹסְדוֹת תֵּבֵל' (foundations of world were laid bare). God's rebuke and breath expose cosmic architecture. Creation undone/revealed?
  Lexical: אֲפִיקֵי מַיִם, מוֹסְדוֹת תֵּבֵל, מִגַּעֲרָתְךָ יְהֹוָה מִנִּשְׁמַת רוּחַ אַפֶּךָ
  Figurative: waters and earth as concealing layers, divine breath as cosmic force

**Verse 17**
**Phonetic**: `yish-**LAḤ** mim-mā-**RŌM** yiq-qā-**ḤĒ**-niy yam-**SHĒ**-niy mim-**MA**-yim ra-**BIY**-m`
God reaches from on high to rescue. 'יִשְׁלַח מִמָּרוֹם יִקָּחֵנִי' (He sent from heights, He took me) - spatial descent to individual. 'יַמְשֵׁנִי מִמַּיִם רַבִּים' (He drew me from mighty waters) - exodus imagery. The cosmic theophany narrows to personal rescue.
  Lexical: יִשְׁלַח מִמָּרוֹם, יַמְשֵׁנִי מִמַּיִם רַבִּים
  Figurative: mighty waters = death/chaos from vv.5-6, drawing from water = birth/exodus

**Verse 18**
**Phonetic**: `yats-tsiy-**LĒ**-niy mē-'ō-yə-**VIY** **ʿĀZ** ū-mis-sō-nə-**'AY** kiy-'ā-mə-**TSŪ** mim-**MEN**-niy`
Enemies identified as 'too strong for me'. 'מֵאֹיְבִי עָז' (from my fierce enemy) and 'מִשֹּׂנְאַי כִּי־אָמְצוּ מִמֶּנִּי' (from my foes who were too strong for me). This admission of weakness contrasts with later victory claims.
  Lexical: מֵאֹיְבִי עָז, כִּי־אָמְצוּ מִמֶּנִּי
  Figurative: strength as relative comparison

**Verse 19**
**Phonetic**: `yə-qad-**MŪ**-niy və-yōm-'ēy-**DHIY** way-hiy-yə-hō-**WĀH** lə-mish-**ʿĀN** **LIY**`
Enemies 'confronted me on day of my calamity' but LORD was my support. 'יְקַדְּמוּנִי בְיוֹם־אֵידִי' (they confronted me on day of my disaster) - temporal specificity. 'וַיְהִי־יְהֹוָה לְמִשְׁעָן לִי' (LORD was support/staff to me) - prop/crutch imagery.
  Lexical: יְקַדְּמוּנִי, בְיוֹם־אֵידִי, לְמִשְׁעָן
  Figurative: support/staff = God as crutch/prop

**Verse 20**
**Phonetic**: `way-yō-tsiy-**'Ē**-niy lam-mer-**ḤĀV** yə-ḥal-**LTSĒ**-niy kiy-ḥā-fēts-**BIY**`
God brought psalmist 'to freedom' (לַמֶּרְחָב - to spaciousness/broad place). Final clause: 'כִּי־חָפֵץ־בִּי' (because He delighted in me) - introduces merit theology. Divine pleasure as basis for rescue.
  Lexical: וַיּוֹצִיאֵנִי לַמֶּרְחָב, כִּי־חָפֵץ־בִּי
  Figurative: broad place = freedom, safety, life (opposite of death's ropes), divine delight = election/favor

**Verse 21**
**Phonetic**: `yigh-**MLĒ**-niy yə-hō-**WĀH** kə-tsidh-**QIY** kə-**VŌR** yā-**DHAY** yā-**SHIY**-v **LIY**`
First righteousness claim. 'יִגְמְלֵנִי יְהֹוָה כְּצִדְקִי' (LORD rewarded me according to my righteousness). 'כְּבֹר יָדַי יָשִׁיב לִי' (according to cleanness of my hands He repaid me). Merit theology introduced - divine response proportional to human righteousness.
  Lexical: יִגְמְלֵנִי יְהֹוָה כְּצִדְקִי, כְּבֹר יָדַי
  Figurative: clean hands = innocent actions, no blood guilt

**Verse 22**
**Phonetic**: `kiy-shā-mar-**TIY** dar-**KHĒY** yə-hō-**WĀH** wə-lō'-rā-**SHAʿ**-tiy mē-'e-lō-**HĀY**`
Self-justification continues. 'כִּי־שָׁמַרְתִּי דַּרְכֵי יְהֹוָה' (I kept ways of LORD) and 'וְלֹא־רָשַׁעְתִּי מֵאֱלֹהָי' (I have not been wicked away from my God). Claim of covenantal faithfulness - kept ways, not wickedly departed.
  Lexical: שָׁמַרְתִּי דַּרְכֵי יְהֹוָה, וְלֹא־רָשַׁעְתִּי
  Figurative: ways = lifestyle/behavior pattern, wicked departure = apostasy

**Verse 23**
**Phonetic**: `**KIY** khol-mish-pā-**TĀY**-w lə-negh-**DIY** wə-ḥuq-qō-**THĀY**-w lō'-'ā-**SIY**-r **MEN**-niy`
Continued self-justification with comprehensive claim. 'כָּל־מִשְׁפָּטָיו לְנֶגְדִּי' (all His ordinances before me) and 'וְחֻקֹּתָיו לֹא־אָסִיר מֶנִּי' (His statutes I did not remove from me). Total awareness and obedience - nothing overlooked or rejected.
  Lexical: כׇל־מִשְׁפָּטָיו לְנֶגְדִּי, וְחֻקֹּתָיו לֹא־אָסִיר מֶנִּי
  Figurative: laws before eyes = constant attention, not removing = holding fast

**Verse 24**
**Phonetic**: `wā-'e-**HIY** thā-**MIY**-m ʿim-**MŌ** wā-'esh-tam-**MĒR** mē-ʿa-ō-**NIY**`
Peak of self-righteousness claim. 'וָאֱהִי תָמִים עִמּוֹ' (I was blameless with Him) - תמים is strong term, perfection/integrity. 'וָאֶשְׁתַּמֵּר מֵעֲוֺנִי' (I guarded myself from my iniquity) - self-discipline from sin. Why 'MY iniquity' (personal besetting sin)?
  Lexical: וָאֱהִי תָמִים עִמּוֹ, וָאֶשְׁתַּמֵּר מֵעֲוֺנִי
  Figurative: blamelessness = integrity/wholeness, guarding = vigilance against sin

**Verse 25**
**Phonetic**: `way-yā-shev-yə-hō-**WĀH** **LIY** khə-tsidh-**QIY** kə-**VŌR** yā-**DHAY** lə-**NE**-ghedh ʿēy-**NĀY**-w`
Repetition of v.21 nearly verbatim - 'וַיָּֽשֶׁב־יְהֹוָה לִי כְצִדְקִי כְּבֹר יָדַי לְנֶגֶד עֵינָיו' (LORD repaid me according to righteousness, cleanness of hands before His eyes). Added phrase 'before His eyes' - God as witness to righteousness. Inclusio completing merit theology section.
  Lexical: לְנֶגֶד עֵינָיו
  Figurative: divine eyes = omniscience, witness

**Verse 26**
**Phonetic**: `ʿim-ḥā-**SIY**-dh tith-ḥas-**SĀDH** ʿim-gə-**VAR** tā-**MIY**-m ti-tam-**MĀM**`
SHIFT to wisdom-style proverbial principle. 'עִם־חָסִיד תִּתְחַסָּד' (with loyal You show loyalty) - divine reciprocity principle. God's treatment matches human character. Begins series of character types and divine responses.
  Lexical: עִם־חָסִיד תִּתְחַסָּד, עִם־גְּבַר תָּמִים תִּתַּמָּם
  Figurative: divine character as mirror of human character

**Verse 27**
**Phonetic**: `ʿim-nā-**VĀR** tith-bā-**RĀR** wə-ʿim-ʿiq-**QĒSH** tith-pa-**TĀL**`
Continues reciprocity principle with pure/perverse pair. 'עִם־נָבָר תִּתְבָּרָר' (with pure You show Yourself pure) and 'וְעִם־עִקֵּשׁ תִּתְפַּתָּל' (with crooked/perverse You show Yourself twisted). Striking claim - God becomes twisted with the twisted?
  Lexical: עִם־נָבָר תִּתְבָּרָר, עִם־עִקֵּשׁ תִּתְפַּתָּל
  Figurative: divine character as responsive rather than static

**Verse 28**
**Phonetic**: `kiy-'a-**TĀH** ʿam-ʿā-**NIY** thō-**SHI**-yaʿ wə-ʿēy-**NA**-yim rā-**MŌTH** tash-**PIY**-l`
Continues with social reciprocity - humble/haughty pair. 'כִּי־אַתָּה עַם־עָנִי תוֹשִׁיעַ' (You deliver humble/afflicted people) and 'וְעֵינַיִם רָמוֹת תַּשְׁפִּיל' (haughty eyes You humble). God champions oppressed, opposes proud.
  Lexical: עַם־עָנִי, עֵינַיִם רָמוֹת, תַּשְׁפִּיל
  Figurative: lifted eyes = pride, divine saving/humbling as social justice

**Verse 29**
**Phonetic**: `kiy-'a-**TĀH** tā-**'IY**-r nē-**RIY** yə-hō-**WĀH** 'e-lō-**HAY** ya-**GI**-yah ḥosh-**KIY**`
Returns to personal testimony with light metaphor. 'כִּי־אַתָּה תָּאִיר נֵרִי' (You light my lamp) and 'יְהֹוָה אֱלֹהַי יַגִּיהַּ חׇשְׁכִּי' (LORD my God illumines my darkness). Light/darkness pair - divine illumination theme. Why lamp specifically?
  Lexical: תָּאִיר נֵרִי, יַגִּיהַּ חׇשְׁכִּי
  Figurative: lamp = individual life/spirit, darkness = distress/ignorance/death, light = life/guidance/salvation

**Verse 30**
**Phonetic**: `kiy-və-**KHĀ** 'ā-**RUTS** gə-**DHŪDH** ū-vē'-lō-**HAY** 'a-dhal-legh-**SHŪR**`
Divine enablement for military action. 'כִּי־בְךָ אָרֻץ גְּדוּד' (with You I rush a barrier/raid a troop) and 'וּבֵאלֹהַי אֲדַלֶּג־שׁוּר' (with my God I scale a wall). Two military metaphors - offensive and defensive/siege warfare. Empowerment theme begins.
  Lexical: אָרֻץ גְּדוּד, אֲדַלֶּג־שׁוּר
  Figurative: rushing barrier = breaking through obstacles, scaling wall = overcoming defenses

**Verse 31**
**Phonetic**: `hā-**'ĒL** tā-miy-**M** dar-**KŌ** 'im-rath-yə-hō-**WĀH** tsə-rū-**FĀH** mā-**GHĒN** **HŪ'** lə-**KHŌL**  ha-ḥō-**SIY**-m **BŌ**`
Divine perfection declaration. 'הָאֵל תָּמִים דַּרְכּוֹ' (God's way is perfect) and 'אִמְרַת־יְהֹוָה צְרוּפָה' (LORD's word/promise is pure/refined). Then shield metaphor returns: 'מָגֵן הוּא לְכֹל הַחֹסִים בּוֹ' (He is shield to all taking refuge in Him). Summary theological statement.
  Lexical: הָאֵל תָּמִים דַּרְכּוֹ, אִמְרַת־יְהֹוָה צְרוּפָה, מָגֵן הוּא לְכֹל הַחֹסִים בּוֹ
  Figurative: perfect way = reliable path, refined word = trustworthy promise, shield = protection

**Verse 32**
**Phonetic**: `**KIY** **MIY** 'e-lō-**AH** mi-bal-ʿa-**DHĒY** yə-hō-**WĀH** ū-**MIY** **TSŪR** zū-lā-**THIY** 'e-lō-**HĒY**-nū`
Rhetorical questions asserting YHWH's uniqueness. 'כִּי מִי אֱלוֹהַּ מִבַּלְעֲדֵי יְהֹוָה' (Who is god except LORD?) and 'וּמִי צוּר זוּלָתִי אֱלֹהֵינוּ' (Who is rock besides our God?). Monotheistic assertion - no other deity can empower like YHWH.
  Lexical: כִּי מִי אֱלוֹהַּ מִבַּלְעֲדֵי, צוּר
  Figurative: rock = stability/reliability

**Verse 33**
**Phonetic**: `hā-**'ĒL** ham-'az-**ZRĒ**-niy **ḤĀ**-yil way-yi-**TĒN** tā-**MIY**-m dar-**KIY**`
Divine girding with strength. 'הָאֵל הַמְאַזְּרֵנִי חָיִל' (God who girds me with strength) and 'וַיִּתֵּן תָּמִים דַּרְכִּי' (made my way perfect). Military preparation language - girding for battle. Way made perfect echoes v.31 perfect way of God.
  Lexical: הַמְאַזְּרֵנִי חָיִל, וַיִּתֵּן תָּמִים דַּרְכִּי
  Figurative: girding = preparation for combat, perfect way = successful path

**Verse 34**
**Phonetic**: `mə-sha-**ŪH** ragh-**LAY** kā-'ay-yā-**LŌTH** wə-**ʿAL** bā-mō-**THAY** ya-ʿa-miy-**DHĒ**-niy`
Animal metaphor for agility. 'מְשַׁוֶּה רַגְלַי כָּאַיָּלוֹת' (makes my feet like deer) and 'וְעַל בָּמֹתַי יַעֲמִידֵנִי' (sets me on my high places). Deer imagery = sure-footedness in mountains. Military advantage in terrain.
  Lexical: מְשַׁוֶּה רַגְלַי כָּאַיָּלוֹת, וְעַל בָּמֹתַי יַעֲמִידֵנִי
  Figurative: deer feet = speed and sure-footedness, high places = strategic advantage, safety

**Verse 35**
**Phonetic**: `mə-lam-**MĒDH** yā-**DHAY** lam-mil-ḥā-**MĀH** **WƏ**-ni-ḥa-thāh qe-sheth-nə-ḥū-**SHĀH** zə-rō-ʿō-**THĀY**`
Military training by God. 'מְלַמֵּד יָדַי לַמִּלְחָמָה' (trains my hands for battle) and 'וְנִחֲתָה קֶשֶׁת־נְחוּשָׁה זְרוֹעֹתָי' (my arms can bend bronze bow). Superhuman strength - bronze bow requires enormous strength.
  Lexical: מְלַמֵּד יָדַי לַמִּלְחָמָה, וְנִחֲתָה קֶשֶׁת־נְחוּשָׁה זְרוֹעֹתָי
  Figurative: divine training = supernatural skill, bronze bow = impossible task made possible

**Verse 36**
**Phonetic**: `wa-ti-ten-**LIY** mā-**GHĒN** yish-**ʿE**-khā **WIY**-miy-nə-khā this-ʿā-**DHĒ**-niy **WƏ**-ʿan-wath-khā thar-**BĒ**-niy`
Divine protection and care. 'וַתִּתֶּן־לִי מָגֵן יִשְׁעֶךָ' (gave me shield of Your salvation), 'וִימִינְךָ תִסְעָדֵנִי' (Your right hand sustains me), and 'וְעַנְוַתְךָ תַרְבֵּנִי' (Your care/humility makes me great). CRUCIAL: what is עַנְוָה here - humility, care, or condescension?
  Lexical: מָגֵן יִשְׁעֶךָ, וִימִינְךָ תִסְעָדֵנִי, וְעַנְוַתְךָ תַרְבֵּנִי
  Figurative: shield = protection, right hand = active power, humility/care = divine attention

**Verse 37**
**Phonetic**: `tar-**ḤIY**-v tsa-ʿa-**DHIY** thaḥ-**TĀY** wə-**LŌ'** mā-ʿa-**DHŪ** qar-sul-**LĀY**`
Freedom of movement granted. 'תַּרְחִיב צַעֲדִי תַחְתָּי' (You enlarge my steps beneath me) and 'וְלֹא מָעֲדוּ קַרְסֻלָּי' (my ankles do not slip). Spatial freedom and sure-footedness - opposite of being trapped (vv.5-6).
  Lexical: תַּרְחִיב צַעֲדִי, וְלֹא מָעֲדוּ קַרְסֻלָּי
  Figurative: enlarged steps = freedom of action, unslipping ankles = stability, reliability

**Verse 38**
**Phonetic**: `'er-**DŌF** 'ō-yə-**VAY** wə-'as-siy-**GHĒM** wə-lō'-'ā-**SHŪV** ʿadh-kal-lō-**THĀM**`
BATTLE begins with first-person military action. 'אֶרְדּוֹף אוֹיְבַי וְאַשִּׂיגֵם' (I pursued my enemies and overtook them) and 'וְלֹא־אָשׁוּב עַד־כַּלּוֹתָם' (did not turn back until destroying them). Intense, active pursuit - psalmist as agent. Shift from passive recipient to active warrior.
  Lexical: אֶרְדּוֹף אוֹיְבַי, עַד־כַּלּוֹתָם
  Figurative: pursuit = reversal of being hunted, not turning back = complete commitment

**Verse 39**
**Phonetic**: `'em-ḥā-**TSĒM** wə-lō'-**YUKH**-lū **QŪM** yip-**LŪ** **TA**-ḥath ragh-**LĀY**`
Complete victory described. 'אֶמְחָצֵם וְלֹא־יֻכְלוּ קוּם' (I struck them down, they cannot rise) and 'יִפְּלוּ תַּחַת רַגְלָי' (they fall under my feet). Three stages: strike, unable to rise, fallen under feet. Total defeat.
  Lexical: אֶמְחָצֵם, וְלֹא־יֻכְלוּ קוּם, תַּחַת רַגְלָי
  Figurative: enemies under feet = complete domination, inability to rise = permanent defeat

**Verse 40**
**Phonetic**: `wat-'az-**ZRĒ**-niy ḥa-**YIL** lam-mil-ḥā-**MĀH** takh-**RI**-yaʿ qā-**MAY** taḥ-**TĀY**`
Divine empowerment for battle restated. 'וַתְּאַזְּרֵנִי חַיִל לַמִּלְחָמָה' (You girded me with strength for battle) - repeats v.33. 'תַּכְרִיעַ קָמַי תַּחְתָּי' (You subdued my adversaries under me). Mid-battle reminder of divine source.
  Lexical: וַתְּאַזְּרֵנִי חַיִל לַמִּלְחָמָה, תַּכְרִיעַ קָמַי תַּחְתָּי
  Figurative: girding = divine empowerment, bowing down = forced submission

**Verse 41**
**Phonetic**: `wə-'ō-yə-**VAY** nā-**THA**-tāh **LLIY** **ʿŌ**-ref ū-mə-san-**'AY** 'ats-miy-**THĒM**`
Enemies turned to flee. 'וְאֹיְבַי נָתַתָּה לִּי עֹרֶף' (You gave me back of my enemies) - neck/back = fleeing posture. 'וּמְשַׂנְאַי אַצְמִיתֵם' (my foes I destroyed). Pursuit and annihilation continue.
  Lexical: נָתַתָּה לִּי עֹרֶף, אַצְמִיתֵם
  Figurative: back of neck = vulnerability, flight, destruction = total victory

**Verse 42**
**Phonetic**: `yə-sha-ū-**ʿŪ** wə-'ēy-n-mō-**SHI**-yaʿ ʿal-yə-hō-**WĀH** wə-**LŌ'** ʿā-**NĀM**`
Enemies cry for help but receive none. 'יְשַׁוְּעוּ וְאֵין־מוֹשִׁיעַ' (they cried out but no savior) - 'עַל־יְהֹוָה וְלֹא עָנָם' (to LORD but He did not answer). SHOCKING: enemies cry to YHWH but God refuses. Divine partisanship explicit.
  Lexical: יְשַׁוְּעוּ וְאֵין־מוֹשִׁיעַ, עַל־יְהֹוָה וְלֹא עָנָם
  Figurative: divine silence = judgment, unanswered prayer = divine rejection

**Verse 43**
**Phonetic**: `wə-'esh-ḥā-**QĒM** kə-ʿā-**FĀR** ʿal-pə-nēy-**RŪ**-aḥ kə-**TIY**-t ḥū-**TSŌTH** 'a-riy-**QĒM**`
Graphic annihilation imagery. 'וְאֶשְׁחָקֵם כְּעָפָר עַל־פְּנֵי־רוּחַ' (I ground them fine as dust before wind) and 'כְּטִיט חוּצוֹת אֲרִיקֵם' (as mud of streets I emptied them out). Enemies reduced to nothing - dust and mud.
  Lexical: וְאֶשְׁחָקֵם כְּעָפָר, כְּטִיט חוּצוֹת אֲרִיקֵם
  Figurative: dust before wind = scattered, gone, street mud = contemptible, trampled substance

**Verse 44**
**Phonetic**: `tə-fal-ltē-**NIY** mē-riy-**VĒY** **ʿĀM** tə-siy-mē-**NIY** lə-**RŌ'**-sh gō-**YIM** **ʿAM** lō'-yā-**DHAʿ**-tiy ya-ʿav-**DHŪ**-niy`
DRAMATIC expansion of scope. 'תְּפַלְּטֵנִי מֵרִיבֵי עָם' (You rescued me from strife of people) and 'תְּשִׂימֵנִי לְרֹאשׁ גּוֹיִם' (You set me as head of nations). From personal enemies to international dominion. 'עַם לֹא־יָדַעְתִּי יַעַבְדוּנִי' (people I did not know serve me).
  Lexical: מֵרִיבֵי עָם, לְרֹאשׁ גּוֹיִם, עַם לֹא־יָדַעְתִּי יַעַבְדוּנִי
  Figurative: head of nations = supreme authority, service = submission, vassalage

**Verse 45**
**Phonetic**: `lə-shē-**MAʿ** 'ō-**ZEN** yish-**SHĀ**-mə-ʿū **LIY** bə-nēy-nē-**KHĀR** yə-kha-ḥa-shū-**LIY**`
Foreign submission described. 'לְשֵׁמַע אֹזֶן יִשָּׁמְעוּ לִי' (at hearing of ear they obey me) - instant submission by reputation. 'בְּנֵי־נֵכָר יְכַחֲשׁוּ־לִי' (foreign peoples cower/cringe to me). Submission without battle - reputation alone.
  Lexical: לְשֵׁמַע אֹזֶן יִשָּׁמְעוּ, בְּנֵי־נֵכָר יְכַחֲשׁוּ־לִי
  Figurative: hearing alone = power of reputation, cowering = forced, insincere submission?

**Verse 46**
**Phonetic**: `bə-nēy-nē-**KHĀR** yi-**BŌ**-lū wə-yaḥ-**RGHŪ** mim-mis-grō-thēy-**HEM**`
Foreign peoples lose heart. 'בְּנֵי־נֵכָר יִבֹּלוּ' (foreign peoples fade/wither) and 'וְיַחְרְגוּ מִמִּסְגְּרוֹתֵיהֶם' (come trembling from their strongholds). Psychological defeat - they lose courage and emerge from fortresses in fear.
  Lexical: יִבֹּלוּ, וְיַחְרְגוּ מִמִּסְגְּרוֹתֵיהֶם
  Figurative: withering = loss of courage, leaving strongholds = surrender, submission

**Verse 47**
**Phonetic**: `ḥay-yə-hō-**WĀH** ū-vā-**RŪ**-khə tsū-**RIY** wə-yā-**RŪM** 'e-lō-**HĒY** yish-**ʿIY**`
Renewed praise after victory. 'חַי־יְהֹוָה' (LORD lives!) - exclamation. 'וּבָרוּךְ צוּרִי' (blessed is my rock) - returns to rock metaphor from v.3. 'וְיָרוּם אֱלוֹהֵי יִשְׁעִי' (exalted be God of my salvation). Doxology validated by experience.
  Lexical: חַי־יְהֹוָה, וּבָרוּךְ צוּרִי, אֱלוֹהֵי יִשְׁעִי
  Figurative: living God vs. dead idols, rock = stability, proven reliability

**Verse 48**
**Phonetic**: `hā-**'ĒL** han-nō-**THĒN** nə-qā-**MŌTH** **LIY** way-yadh-**BĒR** ʿam-**MIY**-m taḥ-**TĀY**`
Divine vindication stated. 'הָאֵל הַנּוֹתֵן נְקָמוֹת לִי' (God who gives vengeance for me) and 'וַיַּדְבֵּר עַמִּים תַּחְתָּי' (subdues peoples under me). Divine action on king's behalf - vindication and subjugation.
  Lexical: הַנּוֹתֵן נְקָמוֹת לִי, וַיַּדְבֵּר עַמִּים תַּחְתָּי
  Figurative: vengeance = vindication, justice, under me = subjugation

**Verse 49**
**Phonetic**: `mə-fal-**LTIY** mē-'ō-yə-**VĀY** **'AF** min-qā-**MAY** tə-rō-mə-**MĒ**-niy mē-**'IY**-sh ḥā-**MĀS** tats-tsiy-**LĒ**-niy`
Summary of deliverance. 'מְפַלְּטִי מֵאֹיְבָי' (my deliverer from enemies) - returns to opening epithets. 'אַף מִן־קָמַי תְּרוֹמְמֵנִי' (from adversaries You raise me) - elevation above enemies. 'מֵאִישׁ חָמָס תַּצִּילֵנִי' (from violent man You rescue me). Three aspects: deliverance, elevation, rescue.
  Lexical: מְפַלְּטִי, תְּרוֹמְמֵנִי, מֵאִישׁ חָמָס
  Figurative: elevation = status, victory, violent man = paradigmatic oppressor

**Verse 50**
**Phonetic**: `ʿal-**KĒN**  'ō-dhə-**KHĀ** va-gō-**YIM**  yə-hō-**WĀH** ū-lə-shim-**KHĀ** 'a-zam-**MĒ**-rāh`
Vow of international praise. 'עַל־כֵּן אוֹדְךָ בַגּוֹיִם יְהֹוָה' (therefore I praise You among nations, LORD) and 'וּלְשִׁמְךָ אֲזַמֵּרָה' (to Your name I sing). Personal testimony becomes public, international proclamation.
  Lexical: עַל־כֵּן אוֹדְךָ בַגּוֹיִם, אֲזַמֵּרָה
  Figurative: name = divine reputation/character

**Verse 51**
**Phonetic**: `magh-**DIL** yə-shū-**ʿŌTH** mal-**KŌ** wə-**ʿŌ**-seh **ḤE**-sedh  lim-shiy-**ḤŌ** lə-dhā-**WIDH** ū-lə-zar-**ʿŌ** ʿadh-ʿō-**LĀM** **F**`
CLIMACTIC conclusion with eternal dynasty promise. 'מַגְדִּיל יְשׁוּעוֹת מַלְכּוֹ' (He magnifies salvations of His king) - royal title explicit. 'וְעֹשֶׂה חֶסֶד לִמְשִׁיחוֹ' (shows covenant loyalty to His anointed) - מָשִׁיחַ (messiah/anointed). 'לְדָוִד וּלְזַרְעוֹ עַד־עוֹלָם' (to David and his seed forever). Eternal promise.
  Lexical: מַגְדִּיל יְשׁוּעוֹת מַלְכּוֹ, וְעֹשֶׂה חֶסֶד לִמְשִׁיחוֹ, לְדָוִד וּלְזַרְעוֹ עַד־עוֹלָם
  Figurative: anointed = special status, seed = dynasty, descendants, forever = eternal covenant

**Thematic Threads Across Verses:**
  - Dramatic scale escalation: personal crisis (vv.4-6) → cosmic theophany (vv.8-16) → personal rescue (vv.17-20) → international dominion (vv.44-46) → eternal dynasty (v.51)
  - Tension between divine agency and human merit unresolved: God empowers (vv.32-37) yet king claims righteousness (vv.21-25) as basis
  - Nine divine epithets (v.3) and nine-verse theophany (vv.8-16) suggest symbolic completeness - why nine specifically?
  - Rock metaphor frames psalm (v.3, v.47) creating inclusio - experience validates theology
  - Warfare imagery progression: trapped by Death (vv.5-6) → empowered by God (vv.32-37) → victorious warrior (vv.38-43) → international emperor (vv.44-46)
  - Spatial movement: constriction (v.7 צר) → spaciousness (v.20 מרחב) → elevation (v.49 רומם) → under feet (v.39, 40, 41)
  - Voice/sound motif: psalmist's cry (v.7) → God's thunder (v.14) → enemy's unanswered cry (v.42) → international praise (v.50)
  - Body part imagery: hands (righteousness v.21, trained v.35), feet (deer v.34, enemies under v.39), eyes (God's v.25, haughty v.28), ears (God's v.7, hearing alone v.45)
  - Light/darkness pattern: darkness of crisis (v.12 God's concealment) → light granted (v.29) → illumination (נגה v.13 brightness)
  - Water imagery: death's torrents (v.5) → mighty waters rescue (v.17) → exposed cosmic waters (v.16) - chaos to creation
  - Shift from passive recipient (rescued vv.17-20) to active agent (I pursued v.38) to passive again (You made king v.51) - complex agency
  - Wisdom interlude (vv.26-28) universalizes personal case into cosmic principle of divine reciprocity
  - Divine names alternate: YHWH (19x), El/Elohim, Elyon (v.14) - theological significance of variation?
  - Righteousness section (vv.21-25) framed by identical verses (v.21, v.25) creates emphatic repetition - merit theology cannot be ignored
  - International scope introduced only at end (vv.44-50) - telescoping effect from Saul to nations to forever


**Interesting Questions** (from Micro Analyst):
  1. Why does the psalm use עַנְוַתְךָ (Your humility/care/condescension) in v.36 to describe what makes the king great - what are the theological implications of divine 'humility'?
  2. What is the identity and significance of בְלִיַּעַל in v.5 - deity, personified chaos, moral abstraction, or something else?
  3. How should the righteousness claims (vv.21-25) be reconciled with grace theology and David's known sins - royal ideology, pre-fall timeframe, or different theological framework?
  4. Why does the nine-epithet cascade in v.3 use both סֶלַע and צוּר for 'rock' - are these synonyms or do they carry distinct nuances?
  5. What is the function and meaning of the rare verb דָּאָה (glide/soar) in v.11 when describing God riding the cherub?
  6. Why do enemies cry to YHWH in v.42 yet receive no answer - are they Israelites, polytheists, or is this making a theological point about divine favoritism?
  7. How does the 'wisdom interlude' (vv.26-28) function rhetorically within a royal victory psalm - generic mixing or integrated royal-wisdom ideology?
  8. What does the phrase 'horn of salvation' (קֶרֶן־יִשְׁעִי) in v.3 signify - animal strength, royal imagery, or cultic symbolism?
  9. Why does v.27 claim God 'becomes twisted' (תִּתְפַּתָּל) with the crooked - what does this say about divine character and immutability?
  10. How should מֵהֵיכָלוֹ (from His temple) in v.7 be understood temporally and spatially - heavenly temple, anachronistic Jerusalem reference, or cosmic palace?
  11. What is the relationship between the cosmic waters in v.16 (אֲפִיקֵי מַיִם exposed) and the mighty waters of v.17 (drawn from מַיִם רַבִּים) - same or different waters?
  12. Why does the theophany (vv.8-16) occupy nine verses - is this symbolic, traditional form, or rhetorical necessity?
  13. How do the specific variants between Psalm 18 and 2 Samuel 22 illuminate liturgical use, textual transmission, or theological development?
  14. What does גְּדוּד mean in v.30 ('rush a גדוד') - barrier, troop, raiding party - and how does this affect interpretation?
  15. Why shift from אֶרְחָמְךָ (visceral womb-love) in v.2 to more standard praise language - what does this opening emotional intensity establish?
  16. How should the graphic violence of vv.38-43 be interpreted - ancient Near Eastern victory rhetoric convention, literal battlefield report, or theological statement?
  17. What is the significance of מָשִׁיחַ (messiah/anointed) in v.51 - referring to David himself, each successive king, or eschatological figure?
  18. Why does v.36 add 'before His eyes' (לְנֶגֶד עֵינָיו) when repeating v.21's righteousness claim - what does divine witnessing add?
  19. How does the psalm's movement from Saul (v.1) to 'peoples I knew not' (v.44) to 'forever' (v.51) function - telescoping, liturgical expansion, or historical reflection?
  20. What is the semantic and theological significance of the three fire sources from God's body in v.9 (nostrils/mouth/body) - mythological borrowing or deliberate theology?

**Synthesis Notes for Research:**
LEXICON: Focus on ענותך (v.36 - humility/care/condescension), בליעל (v.5), משיח (v.51), דאה (v.11), גדוד (v.30), יכחשו (v.45), יבלו (v.46), יחרגו (v.46)
CONCORDANCE: Track 'rock' terminology (סלע vs. צור), 'enemies' (9x throughout), divine names (YHWH 19x, El, Elohim, Elyon), righteousness vocabulary (צדק, בר, תמים), משיח usage
SOUND PATTERNS: Analyze alliteration in v.8 (געש/רעש verbs), v.13 paronomasia (עבים/עברו), v.45 wordplay (שמע hearing/obeying)
FIGURATIVE: All storm/theophany imagery (vv.8-16), water imagery (vv.5,16,17), body part metaphors throughout, light/darkness pattern (vv.12,13,29)
PARALLEL TEXTS: Compare with 2 Samuel 22 variants, Exodus 15 (victory song), other theophanies (Judg 5, Hab 3, Ps 29, 68), Ugaritic Baal texts for storm god parallels
COMMENTARY: Focus on interpretive cruxes in vv.5 (Belial), 21-25 (righteousness claims), 27 (God becoming twisted), 36 (divine humility), 42 (unanswered prayer to YHWH), 51 (messiah term)

### RESEARCH MATERIALS

### בְּלִיַּעַל
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בְּלִיַּ֫עַל  

n.[m.] worthlessness (cpd. בְּלִי not, without and יַעַל worth, use, profit)—בּ׳ Dt 13:14 + 20 times; בְּלִיָּ֑עַל ψ 101:3 + 5 times;—the quality of being useless, good for nothing. abstr. אִישׁ (ה)בליעל, אַנְשֵׁי הבליעל, worthless, good-for-nothing, base fellows 1 S 25:25; 2 S 16:7; 20:1; 1 K 21:13; Pr 16:27; = בֶּן־ב׳ 1 S 25:17, בְּנֵי ב׳ Dt 13:14; Ju 19:22; 20:13; 1 S 2:12; 10:27; 1 K 21:10, 13; 2 Ch 13:7; בַּת ב׳ 1 S 1:16 (drunken woman); עֵד ב׳ base witness Pr 19:28; דְּבַר ב׳ base, wicked thing ψ 41:9 (yet cf. 3 infr.), 101:3 (add prob. also 1 S 29:10, so 𝔊 We Dr); דָּבָר … ב׳ (elliptical and in apposition) Dt 15:9. concr. elliptical of אישׁ ב׳ 2 S 23:6 Jb 34:18; כָּל־אִישׁ רַע וּבְלִיַּעַל 1 S 30:22; אָדָם בְּלִיַּעַל Pr 6:12. ruin, destruction: so ψ 41:9 according to De Che al., but v. supr.; יֹעֵץ ב׳ counsellor of ruin Na 1:11; ב׳ alone a man of ruin, destroyer Na 2:1; נַחֲלֵי ב׳ floods of destruction (‖ שְׁאוֹל) 2 S 22:5 = ψ 18:5.

### דָּאָה
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: דָּאָה  

a bird of prey (a hapax legomenon in the Bible, occurring Lev. 11:14).

**Etymology**: [Prob. derived from דאה and so called to its pouncing. cp. the related Aram.–Syr. דַּיְתָא, and Ugar. di̧y, name of a bird of prey, prob. the kite. cp. also דַּיָּה and רָאָה (n.).]

### רָחַם
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: רחם  

to love, have mercy, have compassion. he loved (in the Bible occurring only Ps. 18:2). he had mercy upon, had compassion upon. was shown mercy, was shown compassion.

**Etymology**: [Aram.–Syr. רֽחֵם, Arab. raḥima (= he had mercy, had compassion), Ugar. rḥm (= to be merciful, be affectionate), Arab. raḫima (= was inclined forward, was affectionate), Akka. rēmu (= to have mercy, have compassion), Ethiop. meḥra (a metathesized form) ‘he had mercy, had compassion’).]  

**Derivatives**: Derivatives: רֶחֶם, רַחַם, רַחֲמִים, רַחֲמָן, רָחֵם, רַחוּם, רָחוּם, רִחוּם, רָחִים, מְרֻחָם. cp. רְחִימָא, רְחִימוּ.

### קֶרֶן
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: קֶ֫רֶן  

n.f. (usually in der. senses) קְרָנוֹת Ez 43:15 +; cstr. קַרְנֹת Ex 29:12 +, sf. קַרְנֹתָיו 27:2 +, etc.;—horn: of ram (אַיִל) Gn 22:13, so in Daniel’s vision Dn 8:3(×2), 6, 7, 20, and (of goat) v 5, 8, 9, 21; of oppressors in Isr. (under fig. of rams) Ez 34:21, so of nations Zc 2:2(×3); of רְאֵם Dt 33:17 ψ 22:22 (both fig., cf. 92:11); of Zion under fig. of threshing-ox Mi 4:13; ק׳ בַּרְזֶל 1 K 22:11 = 2 Ch 18:10 (symbol.); seen in Zech.’s vision Zc 2:1. used as oil-flask 1 S 16:1, 13 1 K 1:39 (cf. קֶרֶן הַפּוּךְ Infr.). ק׳ הַיּוֹבֵל, as wind-instr. Jos 6:5 (cf. שׁוֹפָר). קַרְנוֹת שֵׁן Ez 27:15 (from curved shape of tusks). fig., of pers., symbl of strength Dt 33:17, ק׳ יִשְׁעִי 2 S 22:3 = ψ 18:3 (of י׳ as deliverer); others sub 3 or 4; especially as lifted up (רום, as of a lordly animal, cf. Dr 1 S 2:1), denoting increase of might, dignity 1 S 2:1, 10 La 2:17 ψ 75:11; 89:18, 25; 92:11; 112:9, so perhaps 1 Ch 25:5 (v. Kau; > Be blow loudly [cf. 1 c]), + לְעַמּוֹ ψ 148:14 (subj. י׳); also haughtiness, arrogance 75:5, 6; opp. גָּדַע ק׳ hew off horns, i.e. reduce, humiliate, La 2:3 ψ 75:11, pass. Je 48:25, so וְעֹלַלתִּי בֶעָפָר קַרְנִי Jb 16:15; אַצְמִיחַ ק׳ לְבֵית יִשׂ׳ Ez 29:21 is appar. of restoration of might (as ψ 148:14 supr., c. וַיָּרֶם), but אַצ׳ ק׳ לְדָוִד ψ 132:17 of raising up individual ruler of Davidic line. of altar, horn-like projections at corners (26 times), Am 3:14; Je 17:1; Ez 43:15, 20 ψ 118:27 Ex 27:2(×2) + 8 times Ex, Lv 4:7, 18 + 6 times Lv (all P); as sanctuary, refuge 1 K 1:50, 51; 2:28. hill (so in Arabic, peak, isolated hill, cf. ### כָּחַשׁ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: כָּחַשׁ  

vb. be disappointing, deceive, fail, grow lean (NH כָּחַשׁ grow lean; Hiph. convict of falsehood, refute; Aramaic כְּחַשׁ grow lean; כַּחְשָׁא leanness, כְּחִישׁוּתָא leanness, weakness; Aph. as NH Hiph.)— Pf. 3 ms. וּבְשָׂרִי כָּחַשׁ מִשָּׁ֑מֶן ψ 109:24 my flesh hath grown lean (away) from fatness (‖ בִּרְכַּי כָּֽשְׁלוּ מִצּוֹם). Impf. וְיִכָּֽחֲשׁוּ אֹיְבֶיךָ לָךְ Dt 33:29 thine enemies shall cringe (come cringing, i.e. orig. act deceptively, feigning obedience) to thee (cf. also Pi 3, Hithp.) Pf. 3 ms. כִּחֵשׁ 1 K 13:18 Hb 3:17; וְכִ֫חֶשׁ consec. (bef. monosyll.) Lv 5:22 Jb 8:18, etc.; Impf. (בָּהּ) יְכַ֫חֶשׁ Ho 9:2; 3 fs. וַתְּכַחֵשׁ Gn 18:15, etc.; inf. abs. כַּחֵשׁ Ho 4:2 Is 59:13; cstr. Zc 13:4;— deceive, abs. Gn 18:15 (J), Jos 7:11 (JE), Ho 4:2 (אלה וכחשׁ together = false swearing according to WeKl. Pr.; + וְרָצֹחַ וְגָנֹב וְנָאֹ֑ף), cf. לֹא תִגְנֹ֑בוּ וְלֹא־תְכַחֲשׁוּ וְלֹא תְשַׁקְּרוּ אִישׁ בַּעֲמִיתוֹ Lv 19:11 (H); לְמַעַן כ׳ Zc 13:4 in order to deceive; sq. לְ pers. 1 K 13:18 Jb 31:28; sq. בְ pers. et rei Lv 5:21, בְ rei v 22. act deceptively against (i.e. seem to acknowledge, but not really do so), sq. בְ pers. Jos 24:27 (E) Je 5:12 Is 59:13 Jb 8:18, appar. also וְתִירוֹשׁ יכ׳ בָּהּ Ho 9:2 and new wine shall deny her (Isr.; i.e. refuse to acknowledge her as its mistress, not yield itself to her); abs. Pr 30:9. cringe = come cringing (make shew of obedience, v. Niph.), sq. בְ pers. ψ 18:45 (Hithp. in ‖ 2 S 22:45); sq. לְ pers. ψ 66:3; 81:16. disappoint, fail, abs. כִּחֵשׁ מַעֲשֵׂה זַיִת Hb 3:17 the product of the olive hath failed (in sense comp. Ho 9:2 supr.) Impf. יִתְכַּחֲשׁוּ לִי 2 S 22:45 shall come cringing to me (‖ ψ 18:45, v. supr.)

### גְּדוּד
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: גְּדוּד ᴵ  

marauding band (making inroads). troop (a division of the army of Israel). regiment, battalion.

**Etymology**: [From גדד ᴵ (= to cut). [For the sense development cp. Eng. section from L. secare (= to cut), division from L. dividere (= to divide). cp. Phoen. אגדדם (= ring-leader), Ethiop. gedūd (= robber), Akka. gudūdu (= marauding troop), Syr. גּוּד, גּוּדָא (= troop band), whence, according to Nöldeke, JAram., Mand. גֻּנְדָּא, Arab. jund (of s.m.). cp. also גֻּנְדָּה.]  

**Derivatives**: Derivatives: גדד ᴵᴵ, גְּדוּדַאי, גְּדוּדָן, גְּדוּדִי, גְּדוּדִית ᴵ. cp. גוד.

### גְּדוּד
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: גְּדוּד

### צָרַף
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צרף ᴵ  

to smelt, refine; to test. he smelted, refined; he tested, tried; he cleansed, purified; he burned, soldered. was refined (a hapax legomenon in the Bible, occurring Dan. 12:10); was burned. he refined, purified (in the Bible occurring only Mal. 3:2 ff.). was refined, was purified. was refined, was purified; was burned.

**Etymology**: [OAram. צריף (= purified), JAram. צֽרַף (= he smelted, refined, tried), Syr. צְרַף (= he cleaned, refined, purged; he contracted, astringed, was astringent), Phoen. מצרף (= smelter), Arab. ṣarf (= pure, unmixed wine), OSAram. צרף (= goldsmith), Akka. ṣarpa (= silver), ṣarrapu (= goldsmith). This base is prob. related to the bases צרב (= to burn, scorch), and שׂרף (= to burn), so that צרף would mean lit. ‘to purify by burning’, whence developed the other meanings of this base. cp. צרף ᴵᴵ.]  

**Derivatives**: Derivatives: צָרוּף, צֵרוּף ᴵ, צְרִיפָה, צוֹרֵף, צָרְפָן, מַצְרֵף, מֽצֹרָף ᴵ.  

---

### צָרַף
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צָרַף  

vb. smelt, refine, test (NH id.; Ph. מצרף smelter: Assyrian ṣurrupu, refined, ṣarpu, silver, naṣraptu, crucible; Arabic صِرْفٌ is pure, unmixed (especially of wine);* very doubtful is Sab. צדף silver, money, cf. MordtmHim. Inschr. pp. 14, 29 CIS iv, No. 291, l. 1);— Pf. 3 ms. צ׳ Je 6:29, sf. צְרָפָ֑תְהוּ ψ 105:19; 2 ms. sf. צְרַפְתָּנִי ψ 17:3, etc.; Impf. 1 s. אֶצְרֹף Is 1:25, sf. אֶצְרְפֶנּוּ Ju 7:4; Imv. ms. צרופה ψ 26:2 Kt, צָרְפָה Qr; Inf. abs. צָרוֹף Je 6:29; cstr. לִצְרוֹף Dn 11:35, etc.; Pt. act. sf. צוֹרְפָם Je 9:6, etc.; pass. צָרוּף ψ 12:7, etc.;— smelt, refine: Je 6:29(×2) in vain hath he smelted continually (inf. abs.; fig. of purifying people); so smelt away Is 1:25 (acc. of dross; fig.); of silver Zc 13:9 ψ 66:10, כֶּסֶף צָרוּף בַּעֲלִיל 12:7 (all sim.); of אִמְרַת י׳ ψ 18:31 = 2 S 22:31, ψ 119:140 Pr 30:5 (אִמְרַת אֱלוֹהַּ); refine (men, by trials) Dn 11:35 (+ לְבָרֵר, לַלְבֵּן). test Ju 7:4 I will test them (the warriors) for thee (י׳ subj.);—perhaps also v 3 (v. I. צפר); more gen., of י׳’s testing, trying (the hearts of) men (often ‖ בָּחַן) Je 9:6; Is 48:10; Zc 13:9 ψ 17:3; 26:2; 66:10. test (& prove true) a man (subj. י׳’s saying) ψ 105:19. Pt. act. as n. smelter, refiner, hence = goldsmith Ju 17:4; Je 10:9, 14; 51:17; Is 40:19(×2) (but perhaps del. vb, cf. Du Che Marti), 41:7; 46:6 Pr 25:4; as a guild Ne 3:8, 32. Impf. 3 mpl. יִצָּֽרְפוּ Dn 12:10 many shall be refined (by suffering; + יִתְבָּֽרְרוּ, יִתְלַבְּנוּ). Pt. מְצָרֵף as n. a refiner, כְּאֵשׁ מ׳ Mal 3:2 (sim. of י׳), cf. מְ׳ alone v 3 (+ מְטַהֵר כֶּסֶף).
